Joining the Congregation as Nafl After Praying Fard
Ruling on joining congregation after having prayed, with exceptions and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joining a fard congregation as nafl after having prayed fard at home, and identify which prayers are excluded and why.

Core Idea
After praying fard at home, you may join the congregation as nafl for Zuhr and Isha, but not for Fajr, Asr, or Maghrib.

Sometimes a person prays the obligatory prayer at home, then arrives at the mosque to find the congregation in progress. Is it permissible to join them? The Hanafi school provides a detailed answer: it is permissible as a supererogatory (nafl) prayer for Zuhr and Isha, but prohibited for Fajr, Asr, and Maghrib due to specific textual proofs. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is joining a fard congregation after having already performed the fard prayer at home. The general ruling is that it is permissible as a nafl prayer.
2
Note the exceptions
The Hanafi school excludes Fajr, Asr, and Maghrib. Understand the specific reasons for each.
3
Understand the reason for each exception
For Fajr and Asr, nafl is prohibited after these times. For Maghrib, although nafl is allowed after it, three rak'ahs of nafl are not prescribed, and joining the Maghrib congregation would require three rak'ahs.
4
Examine the proofs
The two proofs are: (1) the statement of Ibn Umar that Maghrib and Fajr are not to be repeated in congregation, and (2) the hadith forbidding prayer after Fajr and Asr.
The proofs are ahad (solitary) but accepted by the Hanafis.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) generally permit nafl behind fard for all prayers, but the Hanafis restrict based on the proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلف المفترض [۲۴۵] (۷۵) و من اقتدى بامام ثم علم انه على غير طهارة اعاد الصلوة فیمن صلی فی منزلہ ثم ادرک الجماعة یصلی محصم ص ۹۲ نمبر ۵۷۵ ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الرجل یصلی وحده ثم یدرک الجماعة ص ۵۲ نمبر ۲۱۹) اس حدیث میں آپ نے ترغیب دیدی کہ اگر تم نے پہلے فرض پڑھ لیا ہے پھر بھی اگر فرض کی جماعت ہو رہی ہو تو دوبارہ ان کے ساتھ شریک ہو جاؤ۔ تا کہ یہ تمہارے لئے نفل ہو جائے ۔ تو معلوم ہوا کہ نفل پڑھنے والا فرض پڑھنے والے کی اقتدا کر سکتا ہے۔ وں حنفیہ کے نزدیک فجر، عصر، مغرب میں فرض پڑھ چکا ہو تو دوبارہ نفل کی نیت کر کے اقتدا نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ فجر اور عصر کے بعد کوئی نفل نہیں ہے۔ اور مغرب کے بعد نفل ہے لیکن تین رکعت نفل نہیں ہے۔ اور مغرب کے فرض والوں کی اقتدا کرے گا تو تین رکعت نفل پڑھنا ہوگا۔ اس لئے ان میں فرض پڑھنے والوں کی اقتدا نہ کرے۔ البتہ ظہر اور عشا کی نماز پڑھ چکا ہو پھر فرض کی جماعت ہو رہی ہو تو دوبارہ فل کی نیت کر کے فرض والوں کی اقتدا کر سکتا ہے۔ (۱) اس کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے ان عبد الله بن عمر كان يقول من صلى المغرب او الصبح ثم ادركهما مع الامام فلا يعد لهما (الف) (موطا امام مالک، باب اعمل في صلوة الجماعة، کتاب الصلوۃ ص ۱۱۶) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مغرب اور صبح کی نماز پڑھ چکا ہو تو دوبارہ اس کی جماعت میں شامل نہیں ہو گا (۲) عن ابن عباس ان النبي الله نهي عن الصلوة بعد الصبح حتى تشرق الشمس و بعد العصر حتى تغرب (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب الصلوة بعد الفجرحتى ترفع الشمس ص ۸۲ نمبر (۵۸) اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ صبح اور عصر کے فرض پڑھنے کے بعد ان کی جماعت ہوتی ہو تو ان میں نفل کی نیت کر کے شریک نہ ہو۔
Behind the one praying fard [245] (75): And whoever follows an imam, then knows that he was not in a state of purity, he repeats the prayer. As for one who prayed in his house, then catches the congregation, he prays... Sahih [al-Tirmidhi] p. 92 no. 575, Tirmidhi, chapter on what came about the man who prays alone then catches the congregation p. 52 no. 219. In this hadith, the Prophet encouraged: if you have already prayed fard, and the congregation for fard is happening, then join them again, so that it becomes nafl for you. So it is known that one praying nafl can follow one praying fard. According to the Hanafis: in Fajr, Asr, and Maghrib, if one has already prayed fard, he will not follow them with the intention of nafl, because there is no nafl after Fajr and Asr, and after Maghrib there is nafl but not three rak'ahs of nafl; if he follows the Maghrib fard congregation, he would have to pray three rak'ahs of nafl, so he does not follow. However, in Zuhr and Isha, if he has already prayed and the fard congregation is held, he can follow them with the intention of nafl. (1) The proof for this is the narration: Ibn Umar used to say: Whoever prays Maghrib or Fajr then catches them with the imam, he should not repeat them (footnote a) (Muwatta Imam Malik, chapter on action in congregational prayer, Book of Prayer p. 116). From this narration, it is known that if one has already prayed Maghrib or Fajr, he will not join their congregation again. (2) From Ibn Abbas that the Prophet forbade prayer after Fajr until the sun rises and after Asr until it sets (footnote b) (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter on prayer after Fajr until the sun rises, p. 82 no. 58). From this too it is known that after praying Fajr and Asr, if their congregation is held, one should not join them with the intention of nafl.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the permissibility of joining a congregational prayer after having already performed the fard prayer alone at home. The author begins by quoting the matn from al-Quduri: 'Behind the one praying fard...' The commentary explains that according to the Hanafi school, a person who has already prayed fard at home may join the congregation with the intention of nafl, but this is restricted in three prayers: Fajr, Asr, and Maghrib. For Fajr and Asr, after-prayer nafl is forbidden; for Maghrib, nafl after it is allowed but not three rak'ahs, and joining Maghrib congregation would necessitate three rak'ahs, which is not a valid nafl. For Zuhr and Isha, joining is permissible. The proofs are two: the practice of Ibn Umar (showing that Maghrib and Fajr are not repeated) and the hadith forbidding prayer after Fajr and Asr. The lesson emphasizes the Hanafi position that nafl is allowed behind fard for Zuhr and Isha, and the exceptions based on prohibition of prayer in those times.

Key Terms
اقتداء (iqtida'): Following an imam in prayer
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which of the following prayers can a person who has already prayed fard at home join the congregation as nafl?
A) Fajr and Maghrib
B) Asr only
C) Zuhr and Isha
D) All five prayers

Key Takeaway
A person who has already prayed fard at home may join the congregation as nafl for Zuhr and Isha, but not for Fajr, Asr, or Maghrib, due to time-based prohibitions and the odd rak'ah issue in Maghrib.

Ruling on Praying Behind an Impure Imam and the Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of prayer behind an impure imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying behind an impure imam, explain the Hanafi proof from hadith and the principle of inclusion, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument by identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
If the imam is impure, both imam and follower must repeat—the follower's prayer is included in the imam's validity.

This lesson examines a critical inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of congregational prayer when the imam is later found to be in a state of impurity (janabah or without wudu). The Hanafi school rules that the follower's prayer is legally tied to the imam's, requiring both to repeat. The Shafi'i school, however, considers the follower's prayer valid if the follower was pure. By studying the proofs and responses, you will understand the legal reasoning behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
From the matn and commentary, identify the ruling: both imam and follower must repeat the prayer. Note the principle that the follower's prayer is included in the imam's validity/invalidity.
This is the mufta bihi position with no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Identify the Hanafi dalil
Locate the hadith of Abu Huraira (Bukhari) and the effect of 'Ali (r). Understand how these show that the Prophet and 'Ali invalidated the prayer for both imam and followers.
The text also cites a general principle (qā‘idah) that the follower's prayer is connected to the imam's.
3
State the Shafi'i position and their dalil
Read the Shafi'i argument: the followers are pure, so their prayer is valid. Their proof is a hadith from Dar Qutni stating that the followers' prayer 'has passed' (i.e., is valid). Also note the Shafi'i concept that the imam is not fully responsible for the followers.
The Shafi'i view leads to only the imam repeating.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and thamarah al-khilaf
The Hanafi response is implicit: the hadith cited by Shafi'i is not considered authentic or is interpreted differently. The stronger evidence from multiple hadith and companion practice supports the Hanafi view. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in Hanafi fiqh, the validity of the congregation depends on the imam's purity, whereas in Shafi'i it does not.
This difference affects whether a follower must make up the prayer or not.

(245) (75) If someone follows an imam, then learns that he (the imam) was not in a state of purity, the follower also repeats the prayer. That is, if the imam led the prayer while in a state of major impurity or without wudu, then the imam must repeat, because he performed prayer without purification. But along with that, the follower must also repeat. (1) Because the follower's prayer is included with the imam's in validity and invalidity, as has passed earlier with the rule and proofs. Therefore, due to the invalidity of the imam's prayer, the follower's prayer is also invalid and subject to repetition. (2) There is a hadith: Abu Huraira said: The prayer was started and the people straightened their rows, then the Messenger of Allah came out and stepped forward while he was junub, then said: 'Stay in your places,' so he returned, bathed, then came out with water dripping from his head and led them in prayer (footnote c) (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter on when the imam says 'stay in your places' and they wait for him, p. 89 no. 640). From this hadith, it is understood that if the imam is junub, his prayer is not valid. And in another effect: from Ali that he led the people while junub, so he repeated and ordered them to repeat (footnote d) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on leading in a state of janabah, vol. 2 p. 560 no. 4078; Dar Qutni, chapter on prayer of the imam while junub or hadath, vol. 1). Footnotes: (a) Ibn Umar used to say: Whoever prays Maghrib or Fajr, then catches them with the imam, he should not repeat them. (b) He forbade prayer after Fajr until the sun rises and after Asr until it sets. (c) Abu Huraira said: The prayer was started and the people straightened their rows, then the Prophet came out while he was junub, then said 'Stay in your places,' then went back, bathed, then came out with water dripping from his head, then led them in prayer. (d) It is narrated from Ali that he led the people while junub, so he repeated and ordered them to repeat. (Continuation on next page) ... p. 354 no. 1355) From Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib that the Messenger of Allah led the people while junub, so he repeated and they repeated (footnote a) (Dar Qutni, chapter on prayer of the imam while junub or hadath, vol. 1 p. 354 no. 1354). From this effect and hadith, it is also known that the followers also repeat. Imam al-Shafi'i says that the followers are pure, so their prayer is complete; only the imam has to repeat, because he performed prayer without purification. Their proof is this hadith: from the Prophet: 'Any imam who forgets and leads the people while junub, their prayer has passed (is valid), then let him bathe and repeat his prayer; and if he prays without wudu, the same applies' (footnote b) (Dar Qutni, chapter on prayer of the imam while junub or hadath, p. 354 no. 1353). From this hadith, it is known that the followers are in a state of purity, so their prayer is valid, and the imam must repeat. (2) According to Imam al-Shafi'i, the meaning of congregation is that all are praying together, but the imam is not fully responsible for the followers; therefore, the invalidity of the imam does not necessitate the invalidity of the followers' prayer.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the ruling of prayer behind an imam who is found to be in a state of impurity (janabah or without wudu). The Hanafi position, derived from the matn of al-Quduri, is that both the imam and the followers must repeat their prayer. The reason is that the follower's prayer is legally connected to the imam's — if the imam's prayer is invalid, so is the follower's. The author provides supporting evidence from the hadith of the Prophet (s) coming out junub, and from the action of 'Ali (r) who ordered the people to repeat when he had led them while junub. Then the Shafi'i position is presented: the followers are pure, so their prayer is valid; only the imam repeats. Their proof is a hadith from Dar Qutni that states the followers' prayer is valid. The Shafi'i also argue that the imam is not fully responsible for the followers' prayer. The Hanafi response is implicit: the hadith used by Shafi'i is not accepted as reliable or is interpreted differently. The lesson highlights the practical consequence: in Hanafi fiqh, the validity of the congregation depends on the imam's purity, whereas in Shafi'i it does not.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl (full body wash).
محدث (muhdith): A person without wudu (minor impurity).
اعادة (i'ada): Repeating a prayer that was invalid or incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The follower's prayer is valid; only the imam repeats.
B) Both the imam and the follower must repeat the prayer.
C) The follower should repeat but the imam does not need to.
D) The prayer is valid for both because the follower is pure.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the follower's prayer is legally tied to the imam's; if the imam is impure, both must repeat.

Makruh Actions in Prayer: Fidgeting and Moving Pebbles
Rulings on makruh actions in prayer with Quranic and Hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fidgeting and moving pebbles in prayer, and explain the proof from Quran and Hadith.

Core Idea
Fidgeting and moving pebbles in prayer are makruh tanzih because they contradict khushu' but do not invalidate.

In this lesson, we examine two specific makruh (disliked) actions during salah: playing with one's garment or body, and repeatedly moving pebbles on the ground. The author provides clear proofs from the Quran and Sunnah to show why these actions are discouraged, while also clarifying that they do not invalidate the prayer. Understanding these rulings helps us cultivate khushu' without falling into undue strictness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its category
Read the two rulings: (76) on fidgeting with clothes/body, and (77) on moving pebbles. Note that both are classified as makruh. Determine whether they are makruh tanzih or tahrimi based on the author's language.
The author says 'this does not invalidate the prayer but it is not good', indicating tanzih.
2
Extract the Quranic proof
The author cites Quran 2:238: 'Stand before Allah in devotion.' Understand how this verse implies prohibition of playful actions. The command for khushu' (humility) forbids actions that contradict it.
The Quranic command is general (amm), but the Hanafi school interprets it as requiring focus.
3
Analyze the hadith proofs
Two hadith are cited: (a) the prohibition of tucking hair/garment during prostration, and (b) Allah turns away from the one who turns away. Understand how each hadith supports the ruling on fidgeting. The first shows that managing clothes unnecessarily is prohibited, so playing is worse; the second shows that fidgeting is a form of turning away.
The hadith on turning away is from Sunan Abi Dawud; it indicates that Allah's attention is withdrawn but the prayer remains valid.
4
Apply the reasoning to pebbles
Moving pebbles is considered playing ('abath) and thus falls under the same ruling. However, an exception is given: if the ground needs leveling for prostration, one may move them once. This shows necessity ('udhr) can permit a normally makruh action.
The exception is based on the principle that necessity overrides the disliked ruling.
5
Conclude the practical effect
The author explicitly states that these actions do not invalidate (fasad) the prayer. This is an important point: the prayer is valid but deficient in reward. The student should note that khushu' is a recommended quality, not a condition of validity.
Hanafis distinguish between necessary stillness (obligatory) and recommended stillness; fidgeting only affects the latter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مکروہات کا بیان ہے [۲۶] (۷۶) مکروہ ہے نماز پڑھنے والے کے لئے کہ وہ اپنے کپڑے یا اپنے جسم سے کھیلے۔ (۱) نماز میں خشوع و خضوع ہونا چاہئے ۔ آیت میں ہے تو مو الله قانتین نماز میں عاجزی سے اور ادب سے اللہ کے سامنے کھڑے رہو۔ اس لئے جسم اور کپڑے سے کھیلنا مکروہ ہے (۲) حدیث میں بھی ہے عن ابن عباس عن النبي الله قال امرت ان اسجد على سبعة اعظم لا اكف شعرا ولا ثوبا (ج) ( بخاری شریف، باب لا یکف ثوبه فی الصلوۃ ص ۱۳ نمبر ۸۱۶ر مسلم شریف، باب اعضاء السجود و النهي عن كف الشعر والثوب ص ۱۹۳ نمبر ۴۹۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کپڑے کو بلا وجہ بار بار سمیٹنا مکروہ ہے تو اس سے کھیلنا بھی مکروہ ہو گا (۳) حدیث میں ہے قال ابو ذر قال رسول الله الا الله لا يزال الله عز وجل مقبلا على العبد وهو في صلوته مالم يلتفت فاذا التفت انصرف عنه (و) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الالتفات فی الصلوۃ ص ۱۳۸ نمبر ۹۰۹) کھیلنے میں نماز سے دوسری طرف متوجہ ہونا ہوتا ہے اس لئے مکروہ ہے۔ اس سے نماز تو فاسد نہیں ہوگی البتہ اچھا نہیں ہے۔  ۲۴۷ (۷۷) کنکری کو الٹ پلٹ نہ کرے مگر یہ کہ اس پر سجدہ کرنا ممکن نہ ہو تو ایک مرتبہ کنکری کو برابر کر دے۔ (۱) کنکری کو بار بار ادھر ادھر کرنا کھیلتا ہے۔ جس کو مسئلہ نمبر سے میں منع کیا گیا ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے عـن مـعـیـقیـب قال سألت
Mention of makruh (disliked) actions. [26] (76) It is makruh for the one praying to play with his garment or his body. (1) Prayer should be with humility and submissiveness. In the verse: 'Stand before Allah in devotion' (Quran 2:238). Therefore, playing with the body and garment is makruh. (2) There is also a hadith: from Ibn Abbas from the Prophet: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones, and not to tuck up my hair or garment' (footnote j) (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter on not tucking the garment in prayer, p. 13 no. 816; Sahih Muslim, chapter on the limbs of prostration and the prohibition of tucking hair and garment, p. 193 no. 490). From this hadith, it is known that repeatedly folding the garment without reason is makruh, so playing with it is also makruh. (3) There is a hadith: Abu Dharr said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Verily, Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, continues to turn towards the servant as long as he is in his prayer, so when he turns away, He turns away from him' (footnote w) (Sunan Abi Dawud, chapter on looking around in prayer, p. 138 no. 909). Playing involves turning attention away from the prayer, so it is makruh. This does not invalidate the prayer but it is not good. (77) He should not turn over pebbles unless it is not possible to prostrate on them, then he may level the pebbles once. (1) Repeatedly moving pebbles is playing, which is prohibited in the previous issue. (2) There is a hadith: from Mu'ayqib, he said: 'I asked...' (the rest is cut off).
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings on makruh actions in prayer from the section on disliked actions. The first (76) states that playing with one's clothes or body during prayer is makruh. The author provides three proofs: (1) Quranic verse commanding humility (khushu') in prayer; (2) a hadith where the Prophet (s) was commanded not to tuck up hair or garment, indicating that any unnecessary movement with clothes is makruh; (3) a hadith that Allah turns away from the worshiper who turns his attention away from prayer, implying that fidgeting is a form of turning away. The commentary clarifies that such actions do not invalidate the prayer but are disliked. The second (77) addresses specifically moving pebbles on the ground, which is also makruh, but if the place for prostration is uneven, one may level it once. The proof from Mu'ayqib (probably a longer hadith) is referenced. The lesson emphasizes the importance of khushu' and the prohibition of unnecessary actions.

Key Terms
مكروه (makruh): A disliked action that is not prohibited; doing it is blameworthy but not sinful.
خشوع (khushu'): Humility, devotion, and stillness in prayer.
عبث (abath): Idle movement or fidgeting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on repeatedly moving pebbles during prayer without necessity?
A) It invalidates the prayer.
B) It is makruh, but allowed once if needed.
C) It is recommended.
D) It is obligatory to avoid.

Key Takeaway
Fidgeting and moving pebbles in prayer are makruh tanzih, not invalidating, because they contradict khushu'.

Imam's Impurity and the Congregation's Prayer: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute on imam's janabah affecting congregation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the effect of the imam's impurity on the congregation's prayer, evaluate the proofs (dalil) used by each school, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
If the imam prays while junub, Hanafis say both repeat; Shafi'is say only the imam repeats.

In congregational prayer, the imam leads and the followers follow. But what happens if the imam prays while in a state of major impurity (janabah) or without wudu? Does the invalidity of the imam's prayer also invalidate the followers' prayer? This lesson examines the inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on this exact question, exploring the textual proofs and the underlying principles that lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by identifying what the Hanafi school rules when the imam prays while junub. According to the passage, both the imam and the followers must repeat their prayer.
The Hanafi ruling is based on the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and the principle that the imam's state affects the congregation.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Then, state the Shafi'i ruling: only the imam must repeat; the followers' prayer is valid. Identify the hadith narrated by al-Daraqutni that the Shafi'is cite as evidence.
The Shafi'i reasoning also includes the idea that the imam is not fully responsible for the followers.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school responds to the Shafi'i proof. The passage implies that the Hanafis give priority to their own hadith and may question the authenticity or interpretation of the Shafi'i hadith.
The key Hanafi argument is that the Companions repeated with the Prophet, showing that the followers are also affected.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Figure out how this dispute affects actual practice. For a Hanafi follower, if they discover the imam was impure after the prayer, they must repeat the prayer. For a Shafi'i follower, they would not need to repeat.
This difference has real implications for how congregations handle accidental impurity of the imam.

Page 354 number 1355: From Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, led the people in prayer while he was junub, so he repeated and they repeated. (A) (al-Daraqutni, chapter on the imam's prayer while junub or without wudu, vol. 1, p. 1354, no. 1354). From this athar and hadith, it is also known that the followers will also repeat their prayer. Qa'il (?) and Imam al-Shafi'i say that the followers are pure, so their prayer is complete. Only the imam must repeat his prayer because he led the prayer without purification. Their evidence is this hadith: from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Any imam who forgets and leads the people in prayer while he is junub, then their prayer has passed, then let him perform ghusl and repeat his prayer; and if he prayed without wudu, the same applies.' (B) (al-Daraqutni, chapter on the imam's prayer while junub or without wudu, p. 354, no. 1353). From this hadith, it is known that the followers are in a state of purity, so their prayer is valid, and the imam must repeat his prayer. (2) According to Imam al-Shafi'i, the meaning of congregation is that they are all praying together. But the imam is not fully responsible for the followers. Therefore, the invalidation of the imam's prayer does not necessarily entail the invalidation of the followers' prayer.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the effect of the imam's impurity (janabah or lack of wudu) on the validity of the congregation's prayer. The Hanafi position, derived from the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and the athar of the Companions, holds that if the imam prays while junub, both the imam and the followers must repeat their prayer. The reasoning is that the imam is the representative of the congregation, and his invalidity affects them. However, the Shafi'i school, relying on a different hadith narrated by al-Daraqutni, argues that the followers' prayer is valid because they themselves are pure; only the imam must repeat. Imam Shafi'i's rationale is that the congregation is merely praying together, and the imam is not fully responsible for the followers' prayer. Thus, the invalidity of the imam's prayer does not necessitate the invalidity of theirs. The Hanafi response, as implied in the passage, is that the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib shows that the Companions repeated along with the Prophet, indicating that the followers must also repeat. The Hanafi school maintains that the imam's state affects the congregation because the prayer is performed collectively under his leadership.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl
مقتدی (muqtadi): The follower in congregational prayer
اثر (athar): A tradition from a Companion
حدیث (hadith): A saying or action of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The followers' prayer is valid, only the imam must repeat.
B) Both the imam and the followers must repeat their prayer.
C) The prayer is invalid for everyone.
D) The followers must repeat only if they knew the imam was junub.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imam's state of purity directly impacts the congregation, requiring both to repeat if the imam was impure.

Ruling on Smoothing Pebbles in Prayer: Prohibition and Exception
Ruling with hadith proofs on smoothing pebbles in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on smoothing pebbles during prayer, identify the evidence from hadith, and apply the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
Smoothing pebbles in prayer is makruh; only one motion is permitted if needed for valid prostration.

This lesson addresses a common concern during prayer: what if the ground is uneven? Our text provides a clear ruling based on Prophetic guidance. Repeatedly smoothing pebbles (masḥ al-ḥaṣā) is considered fidgeting and thus makruh tanzihi (disliked). However, if the pebbles prevent a proper prostration, one motion to level the ground is allowed. Let's examine the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is repeatedly smoothing or turning over pebbles during prayer. The ruling according to the Hanafi school is makruh tanzihi (disliked).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Two hadith are cited: the hadith of Mu'ayqib grants permission for 'once' if necessary; the hadith of Abu Dharr prohibits wiping pebbles altogether, emphasizing that mercy faces the worshipper.
The combination of these hadith yields the ruling.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's expression 'once' indicates that more than once is prohibited, while the general prohibition in Abu Dharr's hadith is qualified by necessity. The Hanafi school gives precedence to the specific permission over the general prohibition.
This follows the usul principle that specific (khass) qualifies general (amm).
4
Identify the exception
The exception applies when prostration is not possible without smoothing the pebbles. In that case, one motion is allowed. The necessity must be genuine.
The exception is limited to a single action.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that repeated smoothing is makruh tanzihi; one motion is permissible if needed. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mukhtalaf fih with other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He should not turn over pebbles except if prostrating on them is not possible, then he may level the pebbles once. (1) Repeatedly moving pebbles back and forth is fidgeting, which is prohibited in the previous issue. Commentary
This ruling prohibits the worship from repeatedly turning over or smoothing pebbles on the ground during prayer, as it is a form of fidgeting. However, an exception is made if the pebbles prevent a proper prostration; then one may level them once. The author provides two hadith as proof: the first from Mu'ayqib permits a single motion if needed; the second from Abu Dharr prohibits wiping pebbles entirely, but the exception of necessity is derived by combining them. The Hanafi school holds that the prohibition is makruh tanzihi, and the exception is limited to a single action for genuine need.

Makruhat in Salah: Finger Cracking and Hands on Hips
Ruling with proofs on two makruhat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on finger cracking and ikhtisar in prayer, identify the proof from hadith, and explain why the Hanafi school classifies them as makruh tanzih.

Core Idea
Finger cracking and hands on hips distract from khushu' and are makruh but do not invalidate prayer.

In this passage, the author addresses two specific actions that are disliked during the prayer: cracking one's fingers to produce sound and placing the hands on the hips (often called ikhtisar). These actions are not prohibited in a way that would invalidate the prayer, but they are considered makruh tanzihi because they contradict the spirit of humility and focus. The author cites two authentic hadith to support the ruling, demonstrating how the Hanafi school derives a non-severe prohibition from a general command or prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Acts and Their Ruling
First, note the two actions: cracking the fingers (farqa'at al-asabi') and placing hands on the hips (takhṣur/ikhtisar). Both are ruled makruh (disliked) in the Hanafi school. Think about what 'makruh' means in terms of legal weight.
Makruh tanzih: disliked but not sinful; the prayer remains valid.
2
Locate the Dalil for Each
For finger cracking, the hadith from 'Ali (RA) with an explicit prohibition: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer.' For ikhtisar, the hadith from Abu Hurayra (RA) stating the Prophet (SAW) forbade praying with hands on hips.
Both are ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut), collected in reliable sunan works.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The key is the nature of the prohibition (nahy). In Hanafi usul, a prohibition without a definitive indication of tahrim (haram) indicates karahah tanzihiyyah. The commands are general, and there is no evidence that these acts nullify the prayer.
The Hanafi principle: 'al-nahy yafidu al-karahah la al-tahrim' unless there is an external qarina.
4
Confirm the Absence of Ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is consistent with the majority (including Shafi'i school). There is no significant intra-madhhab disagreement. Both actions are universally considered disliked, though some early scholars permitted ikhtisar.
This strengthens the reliability of the ruling for fatwa.

[24] (78) And he should not crack his fingers nor put his hands on his hips. He should not crack his fingers nor place his hand on his side. Cracking fingers, which produces sound from the fingers, is makruh in prayer. Similarly, placing hands on the hips or waist is makruh. From this hadith, it is known that cracking fingers is makruh. This passage lists two additional makruhat in prayer: cracking the fingers (producing a cracking sound) and placing the hands on the hips (known as ikhtisar). The author provides two hadith as proof: one from 'Ali forbidding finger cracking, and one from Abu Hurayra forbidding ikhtisar. The Hanafi school considers both actions makruh tanzihi because they distract from khushu' and resemble the posture of those who are arrogant or careless. The prohibition is not severe enough to invalidate the prayer.

The Ruling of Sadl (Trailing Garment) in Prayer
Ruling with proofs on makruh of sadl in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sadl in prayer, identify the proof from hadith, and distinguish the exceptional case where sadl is not makruh.

Core Idea
Sadl is makruh in prayer due to risk of awrah and imitating Jews, but not if properly covered underneath.

This lesson examines the prohibition of sadl—draping a garment over the shoulders without securing it—during prayer. The Hanafi school considers it makruh tanzihi based on a prophetic hadith and the twin concerns of uncovering the awrah and resembling Jewish practice. However, a key nuance: if one is already covered with an izār and qamīs, the outer sadl does not attract dislike.

Guided Path
1
Define the act of sadl
Identify the specific posture: placing a garment on the shoulders and leaving both ends hanging loose, without fastening.
This is distinct from ordinary draping or wearing a cloak properly.
2
Determine the ruling
From the text, the ruling is makruh. Specifically, it is makruh tanzihi (disliked, not prohibited).
The hadith explicit prohibition indicates karahah, not tahrim.
3
Examine the proof
The hadith from Abu Hurayra: 'The Prophet forbade sadl in prayer.' This is an ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut) but its indication (dalalah) is qati' in forbidding the act.
Additional reasoning: sadl risks uncovering the awrah and resembles the practice of Jews.
4
Apply the exception
If the worshiper is already properly covered with an izār (lower garment) and qamīs (shirt), then sadl over them is not makruh, as per some scholars and Tirmidhi's narration.
The underlying cause (illa) of kashf al-awrah is removed.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: sadl is makruh unless the person is wearing adequate undergarments that cover the awrah independently.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۴۹] (۷۹) ولا يسدل ثوبه. نہ کپڑا لٹکائے۔ کندھے پر کپڑا ڈال کر دونوں کناروں کو لٹکا ہوا چھوڑ دینا سدل ہے اور یہ مکروہ ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے عن ابي هرير و ان رسول الله الله نهى عن السدل في الصلوة وان يغطى الرجل فاه (ر)(ابوداؤد شریف، باب ماجاء فی السدل فی الصلوۃ ص ۱۰۱ نمبر ۶۴۳ ترندی شریف، باب ماجاء فی کراہیتہ السدل فی الصلوۃ ص ۸۷ نمبر ۳۷۸) بعض علماء نے فرمایا ہے کہ ایک ہی کپڑا جسم پر ہے اور اس طرح لٹکا ہوا ہوتو چونکہ ستر کھلنے کا خطرہ ہے اس لئے مکروہ ہے۔ اور اگر از ria قمیص ہے اور اس پر سدل کر دیا تو مکروہ نہیں ( کما قال فی الترندی فی الباب المذکور ) ورنہ تو یہود کا طرز یہی تھا۔ اس کے ساتھ تشابہ کی وجہ سے مکروہ ہے۔
[249] (79) And he should not let his garment hang down (sadl). He should not let the cloth hang. Placing the cloth on the shoulder and leaving both ends hanging is sadl, and this is makruh. In a hadith: from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade sadl in prayer and that a man cover his mouth. (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi) Some scholars have said that if there is only one cloth on the body and it hangs like this, then because of the risk of uncovering the awrah, it is makruh. But if he is wearing an izār and qamīs, and he performs sadl over it, then it is not makruh (as said in Tirmidhi in the mentioned chapter). Otherwise, this was the style of the Jews, and due to resemblance to them, it is makruh.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This ruling concerns the prohibition of sadl, which is to drape a garment over the shoulder and let both ends hang without securing them. The author declares it makruh based on a hadith from Abu Hurayra where the Prophet forbade sadl in prayer. The commentary adds nuance: if the person is wearing only a single cloth (like a sheet), sadl is makruh due to the risk of exposing the awrah. However, if he is wearing an izār (lower garment) and qamīs (shirt) underneath, and the outer cloth is draped over, it is not makruh according to some scholars, as cited in Tirmidhi. Additionally, sadl resembles the practice of Jews, and imitating them is also a reason for karahah. The Hanafi school thus distinguishes between essential covering and mere style.

Key Terms
سدل (sadl): Draping a garment over the shoulders and letting the ends hang loosely without securing them

Key Takeaway
Sadl in prayer is makruh tanzihi unless the worshiper is already properly covered with undergarments.

Plaiting Hair in Prayer: Makruh and its Proof
Ruling with proof on makruh of plaiting hair in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on plaiting hair during salah and explain the dalil (evidence) that supports it.

Core Idea
Plaiting hair in prayer is makruh because it prevents the hair from prostrating with the body.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling on the conduct of prayer: plaiting (braiding) the hair. The Hanafi school classifies this as makruh tanzihi (disliked), based on a hadith that instructs not to fold back hair or garments during prostration. We will explore the proof and the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, note the act: plaiting or braiding the hair before or during prayer. According to the Hanafi school, the ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked but not prohibited).
The Arabic term used is 'ya'qisu' which means to twist or braid.
2
Locate the Dalil
The evidence is the hadith: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones, not to fold back hair or garments.' This hadith is ahad (single-transmission) but its meaning is clear.
The hadith is attributed to Ibn 'Abbas and is found in the major hadith collections.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The prohibition of folding back hair includes plaiting, as both involve gathering the hair so that it does not naturally fall during prostration. The act of plaiting prevents the hair from being in its natural state of humility.
The key point is that the hair should be free to touch the ground or be near it, symbolizing complete submission.
4
Note the Ijma and State the Mufta Bihi
All major schools agree that plaiting hair in prayer is disliked, so there is no inter-madhhab dispute. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is makruh tanzihi.
Because the hadith is zanni al-thubut (uncertain transmission), it yields a makruh ruling rather than haram.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۵۰] (۸۰) ولا يعقص شعره. اور بالوں کو نہ گوندھے۔ حاشیہ : (الف) میں نے حضور سے نماز میں کنکری پونچھنے کے بارے میں پوچھا تو آپ نے فرمایا اگر ضروری ہوتو ایک مرتبہ ٹھیک کرلو، دوسری حدیث میں آپ نے فرمایا تم میں سے کوئی ایک نماز کے لئے کھڑا ہو تو کنکری نہ پونچھے۔ اس لئے کہ رحمت اس کی طرف متوجہ ہوتی ہے (ب) آپ نے فرمایا انگلیاں مت چٹا ہو جب کہ تم نماز میں ہو (ج) آپ نے روکا کہ آدمی کو کھ پر ہاتھ رکھ کر نماز پڑھے (د) آپ نے روکا نماز میں کپڑ لٹکانے سے اور یہ کہ آدمی اپنے منہ کو نماز میں ڈھائے۔
[250] (80) And he should not plait (braid) his hair. And do not braid the hair. Footnote: (A) I asked the Prophet about wiping pebbles in prayer, and he said: 'If necessary, do it once.' In another hadith, he said: 'When one of you stands for prayer, do not wipe pebbles, because mercy faces him.' (B) He said: 'Do not crack your fingers while in prayer.' (C) He forbade a man from praying with his hands on his hips. (D) He forbade trailing clothes (sadl) in prayer and that a man cover his mouth.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This final ruling prohibits plaiting or braiding the hair during prayer. The Arabic term 'ya'qisu' refers to twisting or braiding the hair. The Hanafi school considers this makruh because it prevents the hair from naturally falling into prostration, which is against the spirit of humility. The footnote at the end summarizes the hadith proofs for the previous rulings as well, serving as a quick reference. Although the footnote mentions multiple hadith, they are all directly related to the makruhat discussed on these pages.

Key Terms
عقص الشعر ('aqṣ al-sha'r): Plaiting or braiding the hair so that it is gathered

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is plaiting hair considered makruh in prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because it is painful.
B) Because it prevents the hair from naturally prostrating.
C) Because it is a sign of vanity.
D) Because it is a Jewish practice.

Key Takeaway
Plaiting hair in prayer is makruh tanzihi; the proof is the hadith forbidding the folding back of hair and garments.
